class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-33"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-34"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-35"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Continental_Airlines&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_history">Early history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Continental_Airlines&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Walter_T._Varney,_founder_of_United_and_Continental_Airlines,_1921.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Walter_T._Varney%2C_founder_of_United_and_Continental_Airlines%2C_1921.jpg/220px-Walter_T._Varney%2C_founder_of_United_and_Continental_Airlines%2C_1921.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="316" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Walter_T._Varney%2C_founder_of_United_and_Continental_Airlines%2C_1921.jpg/330px-Walter_T._Varney%2C_founder_of_United_and_Continental_Airlines%2C_1921.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Walter_T._Varney%2C_founder_of_United_and_Continental_Airlines%2C_1921.jpg/440px-Walter_T._Varney%2C_founder_of_United_and_Continental_Airlines%2C_1921.jpg 2x" data-file-width="446" data-file-height="640" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Walter_T._Varney,_founder_of_United_and_Continental_Airlines,_1921.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Walter T. Varney, founder of predecessors of <a href="/wiki/United_Airlines" title="United Airlines">United Airlines</a> and Continental Airlines, 1921</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Varney_Speed_Lines" class="mw-redirect" title="Varney Speed Lines">Varney Speed Lines</a> (named after one of its initial owners, <a href="/wiki/Walter_T._Varney" class="mw-redirect" title="Walter T. Varney">Walter T. Varney</a>, who was also a founder of <a href="/wiki/United_Airlines" title="United Airlines">United Airlines</a>) was formed in 1934, operating <a href="/wiki/Airmail" title="Airmail">airmail</a> and passenger services in the <a href="/wiki/American_Southwest" class="mw-redirect" title="American Southwest">American Southwest</a> over a route originating from <a href="/wiki/El_Paso,_Texas" title="El Paso, Texas">El Paso</a> and extending through <a href="/wiki/Albuquerque,_New_Mexico" title="Albuquerque, New Mexico">Albuquerque</a>, <a href="/wiki/Santa_Fe,_New_Mexico" title="Santa Fe, New Mexico">Santa Fe</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Las_Vegas,_New_Mexico" title="Las Vegas, New Mexico">Las Vegas, New Mexico</a> to <a href="/wiki/Pueblo,_Colorado" title="Pueblo, Colorado">Pueblo, Colorado</a>. The airline commenced operations with the <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_Vega" title="Lockheed Vega">Lockheed Vega</a>, a single-engine plane that carried four passengers.<sup id="cite_ref-Serling_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Serling-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (August 2010)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Varney was awarded a 17-cent-rate airmail contract between Pueblo and El Paso; it carried passengers as a sideline. Following <a href="/wiki/Air_Mail_scandal" title="Air Mail scandal">cancellation of all domestic airmail contracts</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Roosevelt</a> administration in 1934, <a href="/wiki/Robert_F._Six" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert F. Six">Robert F. Six</a> learned of an opportunity to buy into the Southwest Division of Varney Speed Lines which needed money to handle its newly won Pueblo-El Paso route. Six was introduced to Louis Mueller (who would serve as Chairman of the Board of Continental until February 28, 1966). Mueller had helped found the Southwest Division of Varney in 1934 with Walter T. Varney. As an upshot of all this, Six bought into the airline with US$90,000 and became general manager on July 5, 1936. The carrier was renamed Continental Air Lines (later changed to "Airlines") on July 8, 1937. Six relocated the airline\'s headquarters to Denver Union (later Stapleton) Airport in <a href="/wiki/Denver" title="Denver">Denver</a> in October 1937.<sup id="cite_ref-Serling_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Serling-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Davies_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davies-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (August 2010)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Six changed the name to "Continental" because he wanted the airline name to reflect his desire to have the airline fly all directions throughout the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-COFacts_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-COFacts-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During World War II Continental\'s Denver maintenance base converted <a href="/wiki/Boeing_B-17_Flying_Fortress" title="Boeing B-17 Flying Fortress">Boeing B-17 Flying Fortresses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Boeing_B-29_Superfortress" title="Boeing B-29 Superfortress">Boeing B-29 Superfortresses</a> and <a href="/wiki/North_American_P-51_Mustang" title="North American P-51 Mustang">North American P-51 Mustangs</a> for the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army_Air_Forces" title="United States Army Air Forces">United States Army Air Forces</a>. Profits from military transportation and aircraft conversion enabled Continental to contemplate expansion and acquisition of new airliners after the war.<sup id="cite_ref-Serling_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Serling-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Among those were the <a href="/wiki/Douglas_DC-3" title="Douglas DC-3">Douglas DC-3</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Convair_240" class="mw-redirect" title="Convair 240">Convair 240</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Convair_340" class="mw-redirect" title="Convair 340">Convair 340</a>. The Convairs were Continental\'s first <a href="/wiki/Cabin_pressurization" title="Cabin pressurization">pressurized</a> airliners.<sup id="cite_ref-Serling_7-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Serling-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Davies_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davies-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The airline\'s early route was <a href="/wiki/El_Paso" class="mw-redirect" title="El Paso">El Paso</a> to Albuquerque and Denver, with routes being added during the war from Denver, Albuquerque, and El Paso eastward across Kansas, Oklahoma, New Mexico, and Texas. In 1946 Continental flew Denver to Kansas City, Wichita, Tulsa, and to Oklahoma City, and from El Paso and Albuquerque to San Antonio. Each route included stops in several of 22 smaller cities.\n</p><p>In the early 1950s, Continental began several interchange routes with American, Braniff, and United Airlines. Routes were operated on American from Los Angeles and San Francisco to El Paso continuing onto Continental\'s route to San Antonio and Houston.  Continental\'s Denver to Kansas City route would interchange onto St. Louis with Braniff Airways and United\'s routes from Seattle and Portland to Denver would interchange with Continental\'s routes onto Wichita and Tulsa. These interchange routes continued for many years until Continental was able to secure routes of its own between each city.\n</p><p>In 1955 Continental merged with <a href="/wiki/Pioneer_Air_Lines" class="mw-redirect" title="Pioneer Air Lines">Pioneer Air Lines</a>, gaining access to 16 more cities in Texas and New Mexico. In August 1953 Continental flew to 35 airports and Pioneer flew to 19, but Continental\'s network didn\'t reach beyond Denver, El Paso, Houston and Kansas City until April 1957 when it started Chicago-Denver-Los Angeles, two <a href="/wiki/Douglas_DC-7" title="Douglas DC-7">Douglas DC-7</a>Bs a day each way. Pioneer\'s Executive Vice President <a href="/wiki/Harding_Lawrence" title="Harding Lawrence">Harding Luther Lawrence</a> arrived at Continental as a result of the merger. Bob Six commented on more than one occasion that, "the reason we bought Pioneer was to get Harding." Harding Lawrence implemented several innovative changes at Continental as well as a flamboyant advertising campaign during his ten years as Six\'s protege. During Lawrence\'s tenure Continental grew by 500 percent. Lawrence left Continental in April 1965 to head <a href="/wiki/Braniff_Airways" class="mw-redirect" title="Braniff Airways">Braniff Airways</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-UPI_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UPI-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Six petitioned the <a href="/wiki/Civil_Aeronautics_Board" title="Civil Aeronautics Board">Civil Aeronautics Board</a> (CAB) for longer routes to larger cities, hoping to transform the regional into a trunkline like <a href="/wiki/United_Airlines" title="United Airlines">United Airlines</a>, <a href="/wiki/TWA" class="mw-redirect" title="TWA">TWA</a>, and <a href="/wiki/American_Airlines" title="American Airlines">American Airlines</a>. He was discussing with <a href="/wiki/Boeing" title="Boeing">Boeing</a> for Continental to become one of the first to operate the soon-to-be-launched <a href="/wiki/Boeing_707" title="Boeing 707">707</a>. The timing was crucial, since new routes would justify the 707s, and vice versa.<sup id="cite_ref-Serling_7-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Serling-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Davies_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davies-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1960s">1960s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Continental_Airlines&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: 1960s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Robert_F._Six,_Chairman-CEO,_Continental_Airlines,_1978.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Robert_F._Six%2C_Chairman-CEO%2C_Continental_Airlines%2C_1978.jpg/220px-Robert_F._Six%2C_Chairman-CEO%2C_Continental_Airlines%2C_1978.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="203" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Robert_F._Six%2C_Chairman-CEO%2C_Continental_Airlines%2C_1978.jpg/330px-Robert_F._Six%2C_Chairman-CEO%2C_Continental_Airlines%2C_1978.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Robert_F._Six%2C_Chairman-CEO%2C_Continental_Airlines%2C_1978.jpg/440px-Robert_F._Six%2C_Chairman-CEO%2C_Continental_Airlines%2C_1978.jpg 2x" data-file-width="700" data-file-height="646" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Robert_F._Six,_Chairman-CEO,_Continental_Airlines,_1978.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Robert_F._Six" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert F. Six">Robert F. Six</a>, chairman-CEO, Continental Airlines, 1936\xe2\x80\x931981</div></div></div>\n<p>Continental Airlines had seen a broad expansion of its routes, thanks to a responsive CAB and persistent efforts by Six and Executive Vice President <a href="/wiki/Harding_Lawrence" title="Harding Lawrence">Harding Lawrence</a> (who came to Continental in the Pioneer merger), who both frequently referred to his company as "the Airline that needs to grow."<sup id="cite_ref-Serling_7-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Serling-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Scott_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scott-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (August 2010)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> In 1958 Continental began turboprop flights with the <a href="/wiki/Vickers_Viscount" title="Vickers Viscount">Vickers Viscount</a> on the new medium-haul routes. The CAB permitted Continental to drop service at many smaller cities, enabling the carrier\'s new aircraft to operate more economically on longer flights. In 1960 Continental flew more than three times the passenger-miles it had in 1956. (<i>Aviation Week</i> June 22, 1959: "Continental\'s current re-equipment program \xe2\x80\x94 involving a total cost of $64 million for the Boeings, Viscounts and DC-7Bs \xe2\x80\x94 was launched in 1955 when the carrier\'s net worth amounted to $5.5 million.")\n</p><p>During the late 1950s and early 1960s Six was the airline industry\'s leading lower-fare advocate. He predicted that increased traffic, not higher fares, was the answer to the airline industry\'s problems. To amazement from the industry he introduced the <a href="/wiki/Economy_class#Airlines" title="Economy class">economy fare</a> on the Chicago-Los Angeles route in 1962. He later pioneered a number of other low or discount fares which made air travel available to many who could not previously afford it. One of Continental\'s early innovations was a system-wide economy excursion fare which cut the standard coach fares by more than 25%.<sup id="cite_ref-Serling_7-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Serling-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (August 2010)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Continental took delivery of the first of five 707-124s in spring 1959, and started Chicago-Los Angeles nonstop on June 8.<sup id="cite_ref-Scott_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scott-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Having so few jets, Continental needed radical innovations to the 707 maintenance program. It developed the "progressive maintenance" program, which enabled Continental to fly its 707 fleet seven days a week, achieving greater aircraft utilization than any other jet operator in the industry.<sup id="cite_ref-Serling_7-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Serling-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (August 2010)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Six, not being satisfied with 707 service, introduced innovations and luxe cuisine on Continental\'s 707 flights which were described as, "...&#160;nothing short of luxurious" by the <i><a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Times" title="Los Angeles Times">Los Angeles Times</a></i>, and, "...&#160;clearly, the finest in the airline industry" by the <i><a href="/wiki/Chicago_Tribune" title="Chicago Tribune">Chicago Tribune</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Scott_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scott-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Boeing_707-124_(Continental_Airlines)_LAX.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Boeing_707-124_%28Continental_Airlines%29_LAX.jpg/220px-Boeing_707-124_%28Continental_Airlines%29_LAX.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Boeing_707-124_%28Continental_Airlines%29_LAX.jpg/330px-Boeing_707-124_%28Continental_Airlines%29_LAX.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Boeing_707-124_%28Continental_Airlines%29_LAX.jpg/440px-Boeing_707-124_%28Continental_Airlines%29_LAX.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="695" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Boeing_707-124_(Continental_Airlines)_LAX.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Boeing_707" title="Boeing 707">Boeing 707</a> at Los Angeles, 1967</div></div></div>\n<p>In the early 1960s Continental added flights from Los Angeles to Houston, nonstop as well as via Phoenix, Tucson, El Paso, Midland-Odessa, Austin, and/or San Antonio. In 1963 the company headquarters moved from Denver to Los Angeles.<sup id="cite_ref-Serling_7-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Serling-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Davies_8-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davies-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (August 2010)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> By late 1963 Continental had discontinued service to most of its smaller cities in Kansas, Oklahoma, New Mexico, and Texas with the exception of Lawton, OK and Wichita Falls, TX which continued operating with DC-9 and 727 jets until 1977. Total passenger-miles in 1967 were more than five times greater than in 1960, but 61% of the 1967 total was on unscheduled flights (mostly transpacific charters). During the late 1960s the company disposed of the last of its turboprop and piston-powered aircraft\xe2\x80\x94one of the first U.S. airlines to do so.<sup id="cite_ref-Davies_8-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davies-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Continental replaced the Viscount fleet with <a href="/wiki/Douglas_DC-9-10" class="mw-redirect" title="Douglas DC-9-10">Douglas DC-9-10s</a> and then added <a href="/wiki/Boeing_727-100" class="mw-redirect" title="Boeing 727-100">Boeing 727-100s</a> and <a href="/wiki/727-200" class="mw-redirect" title="727-200">727-200s</a>.  The DC-9 and 727 were to become the workhorses of the fleet from the late 1960s.<sup id="cite_ref-Davies_8-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davies-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The DC-9s were phased out by the late 1970s (although the type reappeared after mergers in the 1980s with an example being <a href="/wiki/Texas_International_Airlines" title="Texas International Airlines">Texas International Airlines</a> DC-9s which were added to the CO fleet); the 727-200 was the mainstay of its narrow-body fleet until the late 1980s. In 1968 a new <a href="/wiki/Livery" title="Livery">livery</a> was launched: orange and gold cheatlines on a white fuselage; and a black "jetstream" logo (by Six\'s friend, the noted graphic designer <a href="/wiki/Saul_Bass" title="Saul Bass">Saul Bass</a>) on the iconic "Golden Tails" of the airline\'s aircraft. The slogans adopted in 1968 and used for more than a decade were, "The Airline That Pride Built" and, "The Proud Bird with the Golden Tail".<sup id="cite_ref-Serling_7-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Serling-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Scott_11-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scott-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> 1960s saw international routes awarded to Continental (to New Zealand and Australia) in the Transpacific Case, but they were cancelled by the Nixon Administration.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Continental_Airlines_B737-200_N7381F.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Continental_Airlines_B737-200_N7381F.jpg/220px-Continental_Airlines_B737-200_N7381F.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="135" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Continental_Airlines_B737-200_N7381F.jpg/330px-Continental_Airlines_B737-200_N7381F.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Continental_Airlines_B737-200_N7381F.jpg/440px-Continental_Airlines_B737-200_N7381F.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1800" data-file-height="1102" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Continental_Airlines_B737-200_N7381F.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Boeing 737-200 with 1968\xe2\x80\x931991 "meatball" logo and livery designed by <a href="/wiki/Saul_Bass" title="Saul Bass">Saul Bass</a></div></div></div>\n<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a> Continental provided extensive cargo and troop transportation for <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army" title="United States Army">United States Army</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_States_Marine_Corps" title="United States Marine Corps">Marine Corps</a> forces to Asian and the Pacific bases. Continental\'s long range <a href="/wiki/Boeing_707-324C" class="mw-redirect" title="Boeing 707-324C">Boeing 707-324Cs</a> were the most common non-military aircraft transiting <a href="/wiki/Saigon" class="mw-redirect" title="Saigon">Saigon</a> <a href="/wiki/Tan_Son_Nhat" class="mw-redirect" title="Tan Son Nhat">Tan Son Nhat</a> airport;<sup id="cite_ref-Scott_11-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scott-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> in 1967, 39% of CO\'s passenger-miles were on scheduled flights. With Continental\'s experience in Pacific operations, the carrier formed subsidiary <a href="/wiki/Air_Micronesia" class="mw-redirect" title="Air Micronesia">Air Micronesia</a> in May 1968, inaugurating <a href="/wiki/Island_hopping" title="Island hopping">island hopping</a> routes between <a href="/wiki/Yap" title="Yap">Yap</a>/<a href="/wiki/Saipan" title="Saipan">Saipan</a>/<a href="/wiki/Guam" title="Guam">Guam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Majuro" title="Majuro">Majuro</a>, Rota, <a href="/wiki/Chuuk_International_Airport" title="Chuuk International Airport">Truk</a>, Ponape (<a href="/wiki/Pohnpei" title="Pohnpei">Pohnpei</a>) and Honolulu.<sup id="cite_ref-Serling_7-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Serling-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> "Air Mike", as it was known, initially operated with <a href="/wiki/Boeing_727" title="Boeing 727">Boeing 727-100</a> aircraft with open-ocean survival gear, doppler radar, and a large complement of spare parts (including tires).<sup id="cite_ref-Serling_7-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Serling-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> A senior mechanic flew on every Air Mike flight until the late 1970s. Air Micronesia operated as subsidiary <a href="/wiki/Continental_Micronesia" title="Continental Micronesia">Continental Micronesia</a> until 2010. In September 1969 Continental began flights from Los Angeles to Honolulu/Hilo and one month later from Albuquerque to Chicago, San Antonio, and San Francisco. In 1970 Continental was awarded routes from Seattle and Portland to San Jose, Hollywood-Burbank Airport, and Ontario, California\xe2\x80\x94all of them growing markets.<sup id="cite_ref-Serling_7-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Serling-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable">\n<caption>Revenue Passenger-Miles (Millions) (sched flights only)\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th></th>\n<th>Continental</th>\n<th>Pioneer\n</th></tr>\n<tr style="text-align:right;">\n<th scope="row">1951\n</th>\n<td>106</td>\n<td>42\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align:right;">\n<th scope="row">1955\n</th>\n<td>221</td>\n<td>11\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align:right;">\n<th scope="row">1960\n</th>\n<td>885</td>\n<td>(Merged April 1, 1955)\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align:right;">\n<th scope="row">1965\n</th>\n<td>1,386</td>\n<td>\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align:right;">\n<th scope="row">1970\n</th>\n<td>4,434</td>\n<td>\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align:right;">\n<th scope="row">1975\n</th>\n<td>6,356</td>\n<td>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>In 1963, Continental denied employment to African-American pilot and Air Force veteran, <a href="/wiki/Marlon_Green" title="Marlon Green">Marlon D. Green</a>. A <a href="/wiki/United_States_Supreme_Court" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Supreme Court">United States Supreme Court</a> decision allowed a <a href="/wiki/Colorado" title="Colorado">Colorado</a> anti-discrimination law to be applied to his case against Continental.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Green flew with Continental for 13 years from 1965 until his retirement in 1978.<sup id="cite_ref-COFacts_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-COFacts-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> His employment paved the way for the hiring of ethnic-minority pilots by all U.S. carriers, an industry milestone which was finally realized in 1977 after <a href="/wiki/Southern_Airways" title="Southern Airways">Southern Airways</a> hired their first minority pilot.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1970s">1970s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Continental_Airlines&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 1970s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>At Six\'s insistence, Continental (with <a href="/wiki/Pan_American_World_Airways" class="mw-redirect" title="Pan American World Airways">Pan Am</a> and <a href="/wiki/Trans_World_Airlines" title="Trans World Airlines">Trans World Airlines</a>) was one of the three launch airlines for the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_747" title="Boeing 747">Boeing 747</a>. On June 26, 1970, Continental became the second carrier (after TWA) to put the 747 into U.S. domestic service. Its upper-deck first class lounge and main deck "Polynesian Pub" won awards worldwide for the most refined cabin interior among all airlines, as did meal services developed by Continental\'s Cordon Bleu-trained executive chef, Lucien DeKeyser.<sup id="cite_ref-Scott_11-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scott-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Continental\'s 747 services from Chicago and Denver to Los Angeles and Honolulu set the standard for service in the western U.S.<sup id="cite_ref-Serling_7-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Serling-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Scott_11-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scott-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> On June 1, 1972, Continental\'s widebody <a href="/wiki/McDonnell_Douglas_DC-10" title="McDonnell Douglas DC-10">DC-10</a> service began. Six had insisted that Continental place a large order for DC-10s with manufacturer <a href="/wiki/McDonnell_Douglas" title="McDonnell Douglas">McDonnell Douglas</a>. This decision again proved prescient, since the publicity associated with Continental\'s splashy 747 service Chicago-Denver-Los Angeles-Honolulu had stimulated increased market share and increased traffic for all carriers. Denver, Houston and Seattle were growing rapidly in the 1970s; the DC-10s took over most flights between Denver and Chicago, Los Angeles, Houston and Seattle, and between Houston and Los Angeles.<sup id="cite_ref-Serling_7-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Serling-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Scott_11-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scott-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Boeing_727-224_N32718_CO_ORD_19.02.78_edited-3.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Boeing_727-224_N32718_CO_ORD_19.02.78_edited-3.jpg/220px-Boeing_727-224_N32718_CO_ORD_19.02.78_edited-3.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Boeing_727-224_N32718_CO_ORD_19.02.78_edited-3.jpg/330px-Boeing_727-224_N32718_CO_ORD_19.02.78_edited-3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Boeing_727-224_N32718_CO_ORD_19.02.78_edited-3.jpg/440px-Boeing_727-224_N32718_CO_ORD_19.02.78_edited-3.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="683" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Boeing_727-224_N32718_CO_ORD_19.02.78_edited-3.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Boeing_727" title="Boeing 727">Boeing 727-224</a> at <a href="/wiki/Chicago_O%27Hare_Airport" class="mw-redirect" title="Chicago O&#39;Hare Airport">Chicago O\'Hare Airport</a> in 1978</div></div></div>\n<p>During the 1970s, Denver served as the airline\'s main hub. The 747s were focused on the Chicago-Los Angeles-Honolulu routes, with one daily round trip through Denver. The DC-10s served large markets (Los Angeles to Chicago, Denver, Houston and Honolulu; and from Denver to Chicago, Los Angeles, Seattle and Houston). DC-9s and 727s predominated elsewhere and added frequencies on DC-10 routes.<sup id="cite_ref-Scott_11-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scott-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Next to <a href="/wiki/Braniff" class="mw-redirect" title="Braniff">Braniff</a>, Continental operated fewer aircraft types (four: the 747, DC-10, 727-200, and <a href="/wiki/McDonnell_Douglas_DC-9" title="McDonnell Douglas DC-9">DC-9-10</a>) during this period than any U.S. trunkline, affording savings in parts, maintenance, and crew training.<sup id="cite_ref-Scott_11-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scott-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> The DC-10 enabled the airline to capitalize on traffic growth in the west. Continental saw market share grow annually in each DC-10 market through the 1970s, until relative market parity was achieved with United, the principal competitor on most of the DC-10 routes. The same innovations introduced on the 747s appeared on Continental\'s DC-10s, including the "Polynesian Pub", but after the <a href="/wiki/1973_oil_crisis" title="1973 oil crisis">1973 oil crisis</a> more seats were needed and the DC-10 pubs were removed.<sup id="cite_ref-Scott_11-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scott-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Continental phased out its 747s in 1978 in favor of the DC-10s (747s would return to Continental during the Lorenzo era, flying Newark to London and Paris). From the mid 1970s until it merged with Texas International, Continental operated only DC-10s, 727-100s, and 727-200s.\n</p><p>From 1961 to 1982, Continental was headquartered at the west end of the <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_International_Airport" title="Los Angeles International Airport">Los Angeles International Airport</a> on World Way West. The facility included the general offices, system operations control, the central maintenance facility, flight kitchen, and Los Angeles crew bases.<sup id="cite_ref-Serling_7-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Serling-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Scott_11-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scott-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (August 2010)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Continental_747.,_Los_Angeles,_1987.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Continental_747.%2C_Los_Angeles%2C_1987.jpg/220px-Continental_747.%2C_Los_Angeles%2C_1987.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="137" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Continental_747.%2C_Los_Angeles%2C_1987.jpg/330px-Continental_747.%2C_Los_Angeles%2C_1987.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Continental_747.%2C_Los_Angeles%2C_1987.jpg/440px-Continental_747.%2C_Los_Angeles%2C_1987.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1232" data-file-height="769" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Continental_747.,_Los_Angeles,_1987.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Continental <a href="/wiki/Boeing_747" title="Boeing 747">Boeing 747</a> at Los Angeles in 1987</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1974, after years of delays and legal proceedings, Continental started flights between Houston and Miami, and on May 21, 1976, Continental was authorized to operate long-sought routes between San Diego and Denver. President <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">Jimmy Carter</a> and Civil Aeronautics Board chairman <a href="/wiki/Alfred_E._Kahn" title="Alfred E. Kahn">Alfred Kahn</a> had been promoting deregulation of the airline industry, which would dissolve the CAB and for the first time in industry history allow U.S. carriers to determine without government supervision where they would fly, and how much they could charge. Continental began flights from Denver to Miami/Ft. Lauderdale and Tampa/St. Petersburg in Florida. That year, President Carter authorized Continental to begin daily round trips between <a href="/wiki/Air_Micronesia" class="mw-redirect" title="Air Micronesia">Air Micronesia</a> destination <a href="/wiki/Saipan" title="Saipan">Saipan</a> and Japan, and approved a route for Continental from Los Angeles to Australia via <a href="/wiki/Honolulu" title="Honolulu">Honolulu</a>, <a href="/wiki/American_Samoa" title="American Samoa">American Samoa</a>, Fiji, New Zealand and Australia. The South Pacific service began May 1, 1979.<sup id="cite_ref-Davies_8-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davies-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Scott_11-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scott-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (August 2010)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> After the 1978 passage of the <a href="/wiki/Airline_Deregulation_Act" title="Airline Deregulation Act">Airline Deregulation Act</a> Continental embarked on a route expansion. October 1978 saw Continental begin flights from the New York area airports to Houston and Denver, and from Denver to <a href="/wiki/Phoenix,_Arizona" title="Phoenix, Arizona">Phoenix</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Scott_11-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scott-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> That month Continental started DC-10 flights between Los Angeles and <a href="/wiki/Taipei" title="Taipei">Taipei</a>, via Honolulu and <a href="/wiki/Guam" title="Guam">Guam</a>. Service between Houston and Washington, D.C., began in January 1979. In June 1979 Continental linked Denver with Washington, D.C., Las Vegas, San Francisco and San Jose and also began Houston-Tampa service.<sup id="cite_ref-Scott_11-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scott-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> The airline suffered in 1979 when the DC-10 was grounded nationwide. Continental Airlines only operated the DC-10 and the 727 at the time, so flights to Hawaii were cancelled during the grounding. By the time of the Texas Air Corp. acquisition in 1981, Continental\'s post-deregulation growth had allowed it to penetrate every major U.S. airline market (and all of the regional markets) from the hubs in Denver and Houston, with the corresponding expansion of facilities at both of these airports. But that growth came at the cost of continuing losses. In Denver, Continental\'s rapid growth provided the final impetus for the construction of the new <a href="/wiki/Denver_International_Airport" title="Denver International Airport">Denver International Airport</a>, which would be completed almost fifteen years later.<sup id="cite_ref-Davies_8-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davies-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Scott_11-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scott-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (August 2010)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Continental_Airlines_DC-10-10_N68042.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Continental_Airlines_DC-10-10_N68042.jpg/220px-Continental_Airlines_DC-10-10_N68042.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="133" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Continental_Airlines_DC-10-10_N68042.jpg/330px-Continental_Airlines_DC-10-10_N68042.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Continental_Airlines_DC-10-10_N68042.jpg/440px-Continental_Airlines_DC-10-10_N68042.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3500" data-file-height="2120" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Continental_Airlines_DC-10-10_N68042.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Continental DC-10 at Narita Airport, Japan</div></div></div><p>\nWhile deregulation allowed Continental to expand into new areas, it hurt the company\'s existing business as consumers were for the first time able to choose lower fares over Continental\'s better service. In 1978 Continental and <a href="/wiki/Western_Airlines" title="Western Airlines">Western Airlines</a>, which held a nearby headquarters and similar fleet, began a nearly three-year attempt to merge.<sup id="cite_ref-Scott_11-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scott-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> </p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Continental_DC-10_model_from_Western_merger.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Continental_DC-10_model_from_Western_merger.jpg/220px-Continental_DC-10_model_from_Western_merger.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Continental_DC-10_model_from_Western_merger.jpg/330px-Continental_DC-10_model_from_Western_merger.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Continental_DC-10_model_from_Western_merger.jpg/440px-Continental_DC-10_model_from_Western_merger.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4032" data-file-height="3024" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Continental_DC-10_model_from_Western_merger.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>DC-10 model used in announcement of planned Continental-Western merger</div></div></div><p>  The route systems would have been complementary, with little overlap; although they both served the Western states, Continental had strength in Hawaii, southern-tier and the <a href="/wiki/Great_Plains" title="Great Plains">Great Plains</a> states; Western\'s strengths were in the California intrastate market, Alaska, Mexico, and the <a href="/wiki/Intermountain_West" title="Intermountain West">Intermountain West</a>. Both airlines served the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Northwest" title="Pacific Northwest">Pacific Northwest</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rocky_Mountain" class="mw-redirect" title="Rocky Mountain">Rocky Mountain</a> states, but along different routes from Los Angeles, Denver, San Francisco, Seattle and Phoenix. The merger attempt failed when Texas Air Corporation interceded with its acquisition of Continental.<sup id="cite_ref-Davies_8-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davies-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Scott_11-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scott-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:2_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>  With the Airline Deregulation Act the world changed for Continental as noted by Smithsonian historian R.E.G. Davies: "Unfortunately, the policies that had been successful for more than forty years under (Robert) Six\'s cavalier style of management were suddenly laid bare as the cold winds of airline deregulation changed all the rules -- specifically, the balance between revenues and expenditures."<sup id="cite_ref-:1_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1980s">1980s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Continental_Airlines&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 1980s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In 1981, <a href="/wiki/Texas_Air_Corporation" title="Texas Air Corporation">Texas Air Corporation</a>, an airline holding company controlled by U.S. aviation entrepreneur <a href="/wiki/Frank_Lorenzo" title="Frank Lorenzo">Frank Lorenzo</a>, acquired Continental after a contentious battle with Continental\'s management who were determined to resist Lorenzo. Management teamed with unions to create a planned Employee Stock Ownership Plan (ESOP) that would have doubled the number of outstanding shares without shareholder approval, thus diluting Texas Air\'s ownership stake and maintaining control of the airline.  But management lost the legal battle to enact the ESOP without shareholder approval and with its 48.5 percent ownership stake, Texas Air could win any shareholder vote.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> During this struggle, in August 1981, Continental Airlines CEO <a href="/wiki/Alvin_Feldman" title="Alvin Feldman">Alvin Feldman</a> died from suicide in his office and was succeeded by George Warde.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> In three letters left to his children, Feldman said he had been depressed since the death of his wife the previous year.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>  Lorenzo became Continental Chairman and CEO in March 1982.  He and his team viewed the company as stuck in the pre-deregulation era and in need of serious changes to be competitive. Continental was experiencing significant financial challenges both before and after Texas Air\'s takeover, and management showed how Continental could not compete and survive with its cost structure.<sup id="cite_ref-Bamber,_G.J.,_Gittell,_J.H.,_Kochan,_T.A._&amp;_von_Nordenflytch,_A._2009_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bamber,_G.J.,_Gittell,_J.H.,_Kochan,_T.A._&amp;_von_Nordenflytch,_A._2009-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>  The pilots union agreed to some cost reductions in mid-1982, primarily through modest productivity improvements, but there was no progress with the other unions. On October 31, 1982, following approval by shareholders of both companies, Continental merged operations with <a href="/wiki/Texas_International" class="mw-redirect" title="Texas International">Texas International</a>, retaining the Continental identity and offering service to four continents (North and South America, Asia and Australia) with a fleet of 112 aircraft. Continental launched its frequent flyer program, initially called Travel Bank, in September 1982, following that of Texas International Airlines in 1979,<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> which was the industry\'s first frequent flyer program, and American Airlines <a href="/wiki/AAdvantage" title="AAdvantage">AAdvantage</a> program in 1981. In mid-1983, Continental relocated its headquarters to Texas International\'s base in Houston, Texas, which resulted in a large expansion of its hub at <a href="/wiki/George_Bush_Intercontinental_Airport" title="George Bush Intercontinental Airport">Houston Intercontinental Airport</a> and extensive new routes to Mexico and the south central U.S.<sup id="cite_ref-Scott_11-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scott-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:AmericaTowerHoustonTX.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/AmericaTowerHoustonTX.JPG/220px-AmericaTowerHoustonTX.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/AmericaTowerHoustonTX.JPG/330px-AmericaTowerHoustonTX.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/AmericaTowerHoustonTX.JPG/440px-AmericaTowerHoustonTX.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1704" data-file-height="2272" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:AmericaTowerHoustonTX.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/American_General_Center" title="American General Center">America Tower</a> in <a href="/wiki/Neartown,_Houston" class="mw-redirect" title="Neartown, Houston">Neartown Houston</a>, Continental\'s headquarters from 1983 to 1998</div></div></div>\n<p>Even with the cost reductions from the pilots in 1982, Continental faced a major competitive threat in 1983 when American Airlines was able to implement two-tier wage structures with its unions.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> American planned massive, rapid growth through new hires at starting pay 50 percent lower than existing contracts and equitable with low-cost, startup carriers and well below Continental\'s pay rates.  American was already one of Continental\'s main competitors, operating a larger hub 250 miles north of Continental\'s southern hub in Houston and the growth that was funded by the lower pay rates was larger than all of Continental at the time.\n</p><p>In 1983, Continental went to its unions to restructure labor costs to compete with the startup carriers and American\'s Plan B labor costs. After 19 months of negotiations, the International Association of Mechanics and Aerospace Workers went on strike in August 1983, even though the company was offering 20 percent pay raises in return for substantial productivity improvements.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>  Continental was able to operate through the strike because many mechanics crossed picket lines and Continental hired new mechanics.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> The company continued to negotiate with the pilots and flight attendants and management gave a final proposal to its pilots in mid-September, which would have provided ownership by the pilots and other employees of 35 percent of the company\'s stock in return for meaningful wage and productivity changes.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> With no agreement, Continental filed Chapter 11 bankruptcy on September 24, 1983 and shut down for three days.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Airline unions fought Continental at every step. On October 1, 1983, the pilots and flight attendants joined the IAM, which was still on strike. When Continental resumed service three days after filing Chapter 11, it initially served 25 cities versus the more than 70 cities previously.  In the initial stages of bankruptcy, with no legal agreement that would allow travel agents to book flights, passengers could only book directly with the airline.  And, with no credit card agreements, Continental could only accept cash for travel.  Continental began offering flights for only $49 for each nonstop segment, raising it later to $75 on any segment. In the federal courts, the unions unsuccessfully sued to stop the company\'s reorganization. They were later successful in working to persuade Congress to pass a new bankruptcy law preventing bankrupt companies from terminating contracts as Continental had successfully done, but the law was too late to affect Continental.<sup id="cite_ref-Buckley_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Buckley-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Delaney_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Delaney-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>  Chapter 11 saved the company from liquidation, but required substantial reorganization, which began immediately. Following bankruptcy, Continental was freed of its contractual obligations and imposed a series of new labor agreements on its union workers, sharply reducing the airline\'s labor costs.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Continental\'s senior management also reduced their salaries to those of the pilots.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> The pilot strike was ultimately unsuccessful due to Continental pilots and new hires who crossed the picket line, and customers who voted with their pocketbook.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Continental became vastly more competitive with the new airline startups then emerging and thriving in the southwestern U.S. By the end of 1984, Continental had grown back to be a larger airline than pre-bankruptcy and that year recorded a $50&#160;million profit.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On April 28, 1985, Continental inaugurated its first scheduled service to Europe with flights from Houston to London/Gatwick. Additional service from Newark to London and Paris started after the airline\'s merger with PeopleExpress Airlines in 1987. With that merger came significant customer service issues, especially in the Northeast, for a period of time. In October 1985, Texas Air Corp. made an offer for a Denver-based regional carrier, <a href="/wiki/Frontier_Airlines_(1950%E2%80%931986)" title="Frontier Airlines (1950\xe2\x80\x931986)">Frontier Airlines</a>, opening a bidding war with <a href="/wiki/People_Express" class="mw-redirect" title="People Express">PeopleExpress</a>, which was headed by Lorenzo\'s former Texas International associate <a href="/w/index.php?title=Donald_Burr&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Donald Burr (page does not exist)">Don Burr</a>. PeopleExpress paid a substantial premium for Frontier\'s high-cost operation. The acquisition, funded by debt, did not seem rational to industry observers from either the route integration or the operating philosophy points of view.<sup id="cite_ref-Scott_11-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scott-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Delaney_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Delaney-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> On August 24, 1986, Frontier filed for bankruptcy and ceased operations. With PeopleExpress losing money, Texas Air announced the acquisition of PeopleExpress on September 15, 1986, at the same time gaining Frontier, whose strong network in the Great Plains and intermountain West reinforced Continental\'s already formidable Denver hub. The PeopleExpress acquisition also provided the option to acquire Terminal C at Newark Liberty International Airport and allow Continental to build a formidable hub in the New York market.\n</p><p>With a reorganization plan that repaid creditors 100 percent, Continental emerged from bankruptcy on June 30, 1986,<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> with improved asset and cash flow positions and a more competitive route structure with routes radiating to every large U.S. city from major hubs at Denver and Houston.<sup id="cite_ref-Scott_11-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scott-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Delaney_30-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Delaney-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Continental also began developing its Midwest hub at <a href="/wiki/Cleveland_Hopkins_International_Airport" title="Cleveland Hopkins International Airport">Cleveland Hopkins International Airport</a> in 1986, just as United Airlines began to transfer its Cleveland hub operations to <a href="/wiki/Washington_Dulles_International_Airport" class="mw-redirect" title="Washington Dulles International Airport">Washington Dulles International Airport</a> in Fairfax County, Virginia. In October 1986, <a href="/wiki/American_Airlines" title="American Airlines">American Airlines</a> senior vice president <a href="/wiki/Thomas_G._Plaskett" title="Thomas G. Plaskett">Thomas G. Plaskett</a> became the president and CEO of Continental Airlines.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> On February 1, 1987, <a href="/wiki/People_Express" class="mw-redirect" title="People Express">People Express</a>, <a href="/wiki/Frontier_Airlines_(1950%E2%80%931986)" title="Frontier Airlines (1950\xe2\x80\x931986)">Frontier</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_York_Air" title="New York Air">New York Air</a>, and several commuter carriers were merged into Continental Airlines to create the  sixth largest airline in the world and became the largest low-fare airline by introducing the industry\'s first non-refundable airfares, initially called MaxSavers. The mergers and the aggressive marketing led to Continental becoming an even larger player in the northeastern markets.<sup id="cite_ref-Scott_11-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scott-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> In July 1987, Plaskett resigned and Lorenzo returned to the position of CEO.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> 1987 saw the creation of Continental\'s OnePass frequent flier program (jointly with <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Airlines" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern Airlines">Eastern Airlines</a>); and, in 1988, Continental formed its first strategic partnership (and the first international <a href="/wiki/Airline_alliance" title="Airline alliance">airline alliance</a> of its kind) with <a href="/wiki/Scandinavian_Airlines" title="Scandinavian Airlines">Scandinavian Airlines System (SAS)</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Scott_11-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scott-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Continental also made a major image change with a blue and gray livery and the "globe" logo that was adopted by the post-merger United Airlines.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:4_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> The airline also elminated first class service\xe2\x80\x94and only the second global carrier to take that action\xe2\x80\x94giving business class passengers the same first class seats, a service change later to be marketed as Business First.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1990s">1990s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Continental_Airlines&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 1990s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Continentalarena002.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Continentalarena002.jpg/220px-Continentalarena002.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Continentalarena002.jpg/330px-Continentalarena002.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Continentalarena002.jpg/440px-Continentalarena002.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1944" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Continentalarena002.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Continental\'s reemergence from its second bankruptcy was signaled by its taking on the naming rights to the <a href="/wiki/Meadowlands_Arena" title="Meadowlands Arena">arena</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Meadowlands_Sports_Complex" title="Meadowlands Sports Complex">Meadowlands Sports Complex</a>, which is located near its New Jersey hub, in 1996. It held these rights until 2007.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Continental_Airlines_DC-10.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Continental_Airlines_DC-10.jpg/220px-Continental_Airlines_DC-10.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="132" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Continental_Airlines_DC-10.jpg/330px-Continental_Airlines_DC-10.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Continental_Airlines_DC-10.jpg/440px-Continental_Airlines_DC-10.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Continental_Airlines_DC-10.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Continental <a href="/wiki/Douglas_DC-10" class="mw-redirect" title="Douglas DC-10">Douglas DC-10</a>. The type was retired in 2001.</div></div></div>\n<p>On August 3, 1990, <a href="/wiki/Scandinavian_Airlines_System" class="mw-redirect" title="Scandinavian Airlines System">Scandinavian Airlines System (SAS)</a> and Texas Air announced that Jet Capital Corporation, which owned controlling interest in Texas Air, was selling its interest to SAS.  Under the agreements, Lorenzo would be leaving the active management of the airline as CEO for the first time in 18 years and would remain on the board of directors for two further years.  At the same time, <a href="/wiki/Hollis_L._Harris" title="Hollis L. Harris">Hollis Harris</a>, formerly President of Delta Airlines, was named Chairman and Chief Executive Officer.\n</p><p>On December 3, 1990,  due to the 1990 <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_invasion_of_Kuwait" class="mw-redirect" title="Iraqi invasion of Kuwait">Iraqi invasion of Kuwait</a> and the resultant <a href="/wiki/Gulf_War" title="Gulf War">Gulf War</a>, which had prompted a dramatic increase in the price of jet fuel, Continental filed for bankruptcy. In mid-1991 Harris was replaced as CEO by Robert Ferguson, who had been a Texas Air executive.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> In November 1992, Continental accepted a $450 million buyout offer from an investor group composed of Air Partners, an investor from Texas led by Texas Pacific Group, and Air Canada.  Under the arrangements, Air Canada would have 24 percent of the voting stock, while Air Partners would hold 41 percent of voting interest in the reorganized Continental.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Continental emerged from bankruptcy in April 1993.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Continental_b757-200_n18119_landing_arp.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Continental_b757-200_n18119_landing_arp.jpg/220px-Continental_b757-200_n18119_landing_arp.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Continental_b757-200_n18119_landing_arp.jpg/330px-Continental_b757-200_n18119_landing_arp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Continental_b757-200_n18119_landing_arp.jpg/440px-Continental_b757-200_n18119_landing_arp.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2762" data-file-height="1843" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Continental_b757-200_n18119_landing_arp.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Boeing_757" title="Boeing 757">Boeing 757-200</a> in 1991\xe2\x80\x932011 livery</div></div></div>\n<p>In March 1993 the airline cancelled its services to nine U.S. destinations and six non-U.S. destinations, including all 24 weekly services between the United States and Australia and New Zealand in addition to its flights between Guam and Australia, effective October 31 of that year.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> In 1994, Continental substantially reduced its jet services in Denver and terminated all turboprop operations, which had been unprofitable, reducing Denver from being a hub to a spoke city.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Continental_b777-200_n69020_arp.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Continental_b777-200_n69020_arp.jpg/220px-Continental_b777-200_n69020_arp.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Continental_b777-200_n69020_arp.jpg/330px-Continental_b777-200_n69020_arp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Continental_b777-200_n69020_arp.jpg/440px-Continental_b777-200_n69020_arp.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2544" data-file-height="1735" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Continental_b777-200_n69020_arp.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Boeing_777" title="Boeing 777">Boeing 777-200ER</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Between 1993 and 1995, Continental experimented with an "airline within an airline" by launching <i>CALite</i>, later renamed <i><a href="/wiki/Continental_Lite" title="Continental Lite">Continental Lite</a></i>, which provided all-economy, low-fare, no-frills service between primarily leisure destinations.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Continental Lite operated with a dedicated fleet of 100 <a href="/wiki/McDonnell_Douglas_DC-9-30" class="mw-redirect" title="McDonnell Douglas DC-9-30">McDonnell Douglas DC-9-30</a>, <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737" title="Boeing 737">Boeing 737-300</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737" title="Boeing 737">Boeing 737-500</a> aircraft, each repainted with the \'Lite\' livery and stripped of its first class cabin. The service was based primarily at Continental\'s existing hub in <a href="/wiki/Cleveland_Hopkins_International_Airport" title="Cleveland Hopkins International Airport">Cleveland</a> as well as a new hub established in <a href="/wiki/Piedmont_Triad_International_Airport" title="Piedmont Triad International Airport">Greensboro, North Carolina</a>. The experiment proved unsuccessful and was dissolved in 1995. Continental\'s short-lived <a href="/wiki/Piedmont_Triad_International_Airport" title="Piedmont Triad International Airport">Greensboro</a> hub was dismantled in the process.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> During this time period Continental was the subject of hostile takeover bids submitted by then Delta Air Lines and Northwest Airlines.\n</p><p>Former Boeing executive <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Bethune" title="Gordon Bethune">Gordon Bethune</a> became president and CEO in October 1994 and was elected chairman of the board of directors in 1996.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Continental went from being ranked last in most performance categories to winning more J.D. Power and Associates awards for Customer Satisfaction than any other airline. <i>BusinessWeek</i> magazine named Bethune one of the top 25 Global Managers in 1996 and 1997. Under his leadership Continental\'s stock price rose from $2 to over $50 per share. <i><a href="/wiki/Fortune_(magazine)" title="Fortune (magazine)">Fortune</a></i> named Continental among the 100 Best Companies to Work for in America for six consecutive years. In his final year piloting the airline <i>Fortune</i> magazine ranked Continental 2004\'s No.&#160;1 Most Admired Global Airline, a title it earned again in 2005, 2006, 2007 and 2008. While at Continental, Bethune created the Go-Forward plan, to fix problems with the airline, which included employee morale, the quality of the product, and the route structure, among others. Bethune began by ordering new aircraft in an effort to convert to an all-Boeing fleet.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Continental_Airlines_767-200ER.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Continental_Airlines_767-200ER.jpg/220px-Continental_Airlines_767-200ER.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Continental_Airlines_767-200ER.jpg/330px-Continental_Airlines_767-200ER.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Continental_Airlines_767-200ER.jpg/440px-Continental_Airlines_767-200ER.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1920" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Continental_Airlines_767-200ER.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Continental Airlines <a href="/wiki/Boeing_767" title="Boeing 767">Boeing 767-200ER</a> upon push-back at <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Tegel_Airport" title="Berlin Tegel Airport">Berlin Tegel Airport</a>, 2010</div></div></div>\n<p>Beginning in 1998, Continental again embarked on a program to expand its international operations. It inaugurated services to Ireland and Scotland, and in October 1998 the airline received its first <a href="/wiki/Boeing_777" title="Boeing 777">Boeing 777-200ER</a> aircraft, allowing <a href="/wiki/Non-stop_flight" title="Non-stop flight">non-stop flights</a> from Newark and Houston to <a href="/wiki/Narita_International_Airport" title="Narita International Airport">Tokyo, Japan</a>, and from Newark to <a href="/wiki/Ben_Gurion_International_Airport" class="mw-redirect" title="Ben Gurion International Airport">Tel Aviv, Israel</a>. Continental in the same year launched partnerships with <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Airlines" title="Northwest Airlines">Northwest Airlines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Copa_Airlines" title="Copa Airlines">Copa Airlines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Avant_Airlines" title="Avant Airlines">Avant Airlines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Transbrasil" title="Transbrasil">Transbrasil</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cape_Air" title="Cape Air">Cape Air</a>, and Continental and <a href="/wiki/America_West_Airlines" title="America West Airlines">America West Airlines</a> became the first two US airlines to launch interline electronic ticketing.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> In 1999, Continental Airlines started service between Newark and Zurich, Switzerland, and from Cleveland to London.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2000s">2000s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Continental_Airlines&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: 2000s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Peter_Max_-_IAH.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Peter_Max_-_IAH.jpg/220px-Peter_Max_-_IAH.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="158" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Peter_Max_-_IAH.jpg/330px-Peter_Max_-_IAH.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Peter_Max_-_IAH.jpg/440px-Peter_Max_-_IAH.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="461" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Peter_Max_-_IAH.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Boeing_777" title="Boeing 777">Boeing 777-200ER</a> "<a href="/wiki/Peter_Max" title="Peter Max">Peter Max</a>" (the colorful aircraft) at <a href="/wiki/George_Bush_Intercontinental_Airport" title="George Bush Intercontinental Airport">George Bush Intercontinental Airport</a> in <a href="/wiki/Houston" title="Houston">Houston</a>, Texas (2006). <a href="/wiki/Downtown_Houston" title="Downtown Houston">Downtown Houston</a> is visible in the background. The livery was removed in the winter of 2007\xe2\x80\x932008</div></div></div>\n<p>On March 1, 2001, Continental launched a non-stop service from Newark to <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong_International_Airport" title="Hong Kong International Airport">Hong Kong</a>, operating over the North circumpolar route. It was the first non-stop long-haul route for any airline with flying duration exceeding 16 hours. The service initiated a brief dispute between Continental, United Airlines and <a href="/wiki/Cathay_Pacific" title="Cathay Pacific">Cathay Pacific</a> over rights to non-stop flights between Hong Kong and New York.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>  On September 13, 2004 Continental entered <a href="/wiki/SkyTeam" title="SkyTeam">SkyTeam</a> alliance along with Northwest/KLM and CSA.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> In 2005, Continental expanded service from Newark to Beijing after being awarded the China route. Among U.S. airlines, only Delta (with its extensive network of legacy routes dating from Delta\'s acquisition of Pan American\'s European network) serves more European destinations than Continental.\n</p><p>In 2005, service to Asia was expanded as Continental introduced daily nonstop service between Newark and New Delhi, India. The success of this Newark-New Delhi route presaged establishment of a second gateway in India with the announcement of daily nonstop service to <a href="/wiki/Mumbai" title="Mumbai">Mumbai</a>. With the establishment of Mumbai service, Continental offers the most nonstop flights by any carrier from the United States to India.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> By May 2006, the carrier\'s passenger traffic surpassed that of <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Airlines" title="Northwest Airlines">Northwest Airlines</a>, and Continental became the fourth-largest U.S. carrier.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wall_Street_Journal" title="The Wall Street Journal">The Wall Street Journal</a></i> reported on December 12, 2007 that Continental was in merger discussions with United Airlines. Of issue would be Continental\'s <a href="/wiki/Golden_share" title="Golden share">golden share</a> held by Northwest Airlines and the divestiture of Continental\'s Guamanian hub. A deal was not "certain or imminent", with the talks being of a preliminary nature.<sup id="cite_ref-wsj_20061213_co_ua_fl_yx_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wsj_20061213_co_ua_fl_yx-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nyt_20061212_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt_20061212-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> In April 2008, at the height of oil prices, Northwest announced a merger with Delta Air Lines. This allowed Continental to buy back the golden share from Northwest Airlines, which it did.<sup id="cite_ref-nyt_20080417_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt_20080417-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Continental then renewed merger talks with United Airlines, but broke them off again that same month.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:NewarkC.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/NewarkC.jpg/220px-NewarkC.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/NewarkC.jpg/330px-NewarkC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/NewarkC.jpg/440px-NewarkC.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1704" data-file-height="2272" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NewarkC.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Continental operated from Terminal C at <a href="/wiki/Newark_Liberty_International_Airport" title="Newark Liberty International Airport">Newark Liberty International Airport</a><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>In May 2008, Continental Airlines sold its remaining 4.38&#160;million share investment in Panamanian <a href="/wiki/Flag_carrier" title="Flag carrier">flag carrier</a> <a href="/wiki/Copa_Airlines" title="Copa Airlines">Copa</a> for $35.75 a share, netting proceeds of $149.8&#160;million. Continental had been a principal shareholder in Copa.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> In June 2008, due to national and international economic conditions, Continental cut 3,000 jobs and the CEO and president had reduced salaries for the remainder of the year. The airline also reduced capacity and eliminated 67 mainline aircraft from its fleet by the end of 2009, retiring all of Continental\'s 737-300s and all but 35 of its 737-500s.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Continental also announced that it planned to withdraw from the SkyTeam Alliance and would join the <a href="/wiki/Star_Alliance" title="Star Alliance">Star Alliance</a> in order to cooperate more extensively with United Airlines and other Star Alliance airlines. The new Continental-United relationship has been characterized as a "virtual merger" in some circles.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In September 2008, Continental announced that it would commence providing seasonal non-stop service between Houston and <a href="/wiki/Rio_de_Janeiro" title="Rio de Janeiro">Rio de Janeiro</a>. The new non-stop flight was timed to provide roundtrip flight connections at Continental\'s Houston hub to more than 160 cities throughout the U.S., Canada, Central America, Europe, and Asia.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Continental renewed its lease for around 450,000 square feet (42,000&#160;m<sup>2</sup>) in <a href="/wiki/Continental_Center_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Continental Center I">Continental Center I</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-houston.bizjournals.com_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-houston.bizjournals.com-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Continental announced that its 4th quarter 2008 net loss widened to $266&#160;million on costs for pilot retirement and reducing the value of its fuel hedges.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> In January 2009, Continental became the first commercial carrier to successfully demonstrate the use of sustainable biofuel to power an aircraft in North America. During the demonstration flight, Continental\'s test pilots successfully conducted a number of flight maneuvers, and the biofuel met all performance requirements as compared with traditional jet fuel. The biofuel blend included components derived from algae and <a href="/wiki/Jatropha" title="Jatropha">jatropha</a> plants, both sustainable, second-generation sources that do not impact food crops or water resources or contribute to deforestation.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> In March 2009, Continental became the first U.S. carrier to inaugurate scheduled service between New York and Shanghai, China, with daily non-stop flights from the airline\'s hub at Newark Liberty International Airport.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:United_Airlines_(Continental_Blue_Skyway_livery)_Boeing_737-924(ER)_N75436.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/United_Airlines_%28Continental_Blue_Skyway_livery%29_Boeing_737-924%28ER%29_N75436.jpg/220px-United_Airlines_%28Continental_Blue_Skyway_livery%29_Boeing_737-924%28ER%29_N75436.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage"
